INDONESIA
Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
Loan and Project Summary
Borrower:                      Republic of Indonesia
Implementing Agency:           Directorate General of Primary and Secondary Education
Ministry of Education and Culture
Beneficiaries:                 School Children and out-of-school youth in the Provinces of DI
Aceh, West Sumatra, Jambi, South Sumatra, and Lampung, with
emphasis on girls and local communities.
Poverty Category:              Program of Targeted Interventions: the project would target student
scholarships, contract teachers, new and alternative schools, and
over 60 percent of quality interventions to rural, mainly poor, areas
and girls.
Amount:                        US$98.0 million equivalent
Terms:                         Repayable in 20 years including five years of grace at the Bank's
standard variable interest rate for currency pool loans.
Commitment Fee:                0.75 percent on undisbursed loan balances, beginning 60 days after
signing, less any waiver.
Financing Plan:                See Table 4.3
Economic Rate of Return:       The estimated rate of return ranges from 15% to 19% percent by
province. High retums were found for both quality and access
components. Retums on quality enhancements were generally
higher than for expansion of access.
Memorandum of the President: No. P-6932-IND
Project Identification Number:  ID-PE-41894

This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission (June-July 1996) to Indonesia consisting of
Brigitte Duces (Senior Operations Officer, Task Manager), Mieko Masuda (Operations Analyst), Stavros
Syrimis (Education Specialist), Dean Nielsen (Education Specialist) and Marsoedi Soedjak (Operations
Officer). Shafiq Dhanani (Economist), and Ibrahim Musa, supported from PHRD funds, also participated
and assisted the mission team. The report also drew on provincial proposals, contributions from the central
and provincial offices of the Ministry of Education and Culture, and inputs from PHRD-funded consultants
(see Annex 19). Valuable comments were provided by Antonio Pereira and Donald Hamilton (EA3PH).
Cynthia Cristobal and Mercedes Bautista assisted in the production of the report. Peer reviewers were Nisha
Agrawal (EA3CO), Bruno Laporte (HDD), Ruth Kagia (EAIHR), and Audrey Aarons (SAIPH). Marianne
Haug, Director, EA3DR and Samuel Lieberman, Acting Chief, EA3PH, have endorsed the report.

Staff Appraisal Report                             I
1. JUNIOR SECONDARY EDUCATION ISSUES IN FIVE
SUMATRA PROVINCES: DI ACEH, WEST SUMATRA,
JAMBI, SOUTH SUMATRA, AND LAMPUNG
A. BACKGROUND
1.1   Over the last 25 years, the Government of Indonesia has demonstrated a strong
commitment to education and as a result, the country has made impressive gains.  The
Government led a substantial school building program and enrollment drive in the 1970s (SD
INPRES) sharply increasing primary school enrollment. The gross enrollment rate stood at 75
percent in the early 1970s but had increased to about 90 percent by the end of that decade. Since
the mid-1980s, gross enrollment rates have exceeded 100 percent. The effort is not complete
because of quality concerns and the need to increase retention rates, but the achievements at the
primary level have been substantial in this diverse and far-flung archipelago.
1.2   The Government of Indonesia (GOI) is looking to future needs, and has reiterated its
commitment to a proactive program in the future with expansion of junior secondary education
as a priority. While maintaining its commitment to primary education, the Government is aiming
for universal basic education (years 1-9) coverage, to meet the human resource requirements for
transforming the economy from one based on natural resources to one based more on industry
and technology.
1.3   The decision to expand junior secondary education participation as rapidly as feasible is
justified for several reasons. First, expansion appears to be a precondition for the rapid growth of
the Indonesian economy in the medium term. Recent research on the development of East Asian
economies indicates that with the achievement of near universal primary education, secondary
(junior and senior) education has had a strong impact on economic growth. Secondary education
plays the role of providing educated entrants to the labor force, enhancing the trainability of
workers, and providing qualified inputs into higher levels of education.
1.4   In this regard, the Indonesian economy and labor market is in the midst of a structural
transformation. Since 1990, there has been a marked slow-down in employment creation in
agriculture and an acceleration in the rate of growth of job creation in manufacturing,
construction, transport and communication. After a period of wage stagnation in the 1980s, there
are strong indications of rising real wage rates throughout the country and an absolute decline in
agricultural employment. If sustained nationally for a decade or so, these trends imply the
absorption of remaining pockets of surplus labor, and as happened in other NICs, the
transformation from an agrarian economy to one relying on the education and productivity of its
workers. Social rates of return to education computed for 1986-92 indicate that returns to junior
secondary education are the highest among the education subsectors.
1.5   The second justification for significant Government attention to junior secondary
education is that the number of school places is still limited relative to demand, despite



2                     Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
substantial outlays by parents and a significant private sector response. This inadequacy is
evident from continued strong demand for generally more costly and often lower quality private
education, large numbers of unsuccessful applicants to schools, the prevalence of double shifts
and large classes in schools, and the use of libraries and laboratories for classroom teaching.
1.6    A third rationale for Government intervention is to assist in removing barriers of cost and
isolation that keep many of the rural poor out of school. In rural Sumatra, for example, total
expenditure per junior secondary student is equivalent to approximately 86 percent of per capita
expenditure in households of the lowest quintile. Junior secondary education is therefore beyond
the reach of many of the poor and near poor. The opportunity costs of keeping children in
school exacerbate the problem. Expansion of junior secondary education, especially in remote
rural and fast growing areas with low enrollment rates will be of direct benefit to the poor,
especially girls who are sometimes disadvantaged in these areas.
1.7    A fourth rationale relates to likely externalities associated with investments in secondary
education. Research from other countries has pointed to externalities such as reduced fertility
and child mortality and improved nutritional status that correlate with female secondary
education. For Indonesia, survey results indicate that a mother's education is an important
determinant of prenatal care utilization: more than 90 percent of women with junior secondary
education use prenatal care, in contrast to only 75 percent of women with some primary
education. Furthermore, analysis of nutritional status in Indonesia finds a strong effect of
maternal education on children's nutritional status: for women with between zero and five years
of education, an additional year of education has no impact on the child's nutritional status,
while, among women with six to twelve years of education (or more), an additional year of
education improves nutritional status significantly.
1.8    The role of the Government would therefore be to (a) help expand the number of school
places through a combination of direct investment and measures to stimulate further private
sector/community participation in the provision of school places; (b) improve the teaching -
learning quality of the existing and new junior secondary schools; and (c) ensure that the poor
are given increased opportunity to participate in the expansion process.
Sector Financing Plan
1.9    Achieving universal nine-year basic education will require significant additional public
resources to be directed to the education sector overall and to basic education in particular.
Estimates of the total costs of the junior secondary expansion program come to approximately
$7.6 billion to be implemented over a 15-year period. The cost estimates assume that the private
sector will maintain its share of total enrollment of about 40 percent. Additional financing of an
average $120 million during the next ten years of expansion (assuming 6 percent p.a. growth of
the budget) would be required, implying that the budgetary allocation to junior secondary
education would need to increase from the current 13 percent of the education budget to
approximately 16 percent in the next few years. Overall, Indonesia would need to increase its
allocation to education as a proportion of GDP by approximately 0.2 percent (this does not take
into account, however, increases in other education subsectors, which may equally be required).



StaffAppraisal Report                               3
1.10 Government has demonstrated its commitment to expansion of junior secondary
education through increases in its education investments. In its most recent budget allocations
(1996/97) the education sector budget was increased by 20 percent, one of the highest increases
amongst all sectors. The budget for junior secondary education saw a 24 percent rise over last
year's budget allocation. GOI is also requesting help from donors, including the World Bank, to
close the financing gap.
Structure of the Education System
1.11   The Indonesian education system consists of a six-year primary school cycle, junior and
senior secondary school cycles of three years each, and a postsecondary system ranging from one
to six years. Primary education is largely the domain of Government: nearly five out of six
pupils are enrolled in public schools. Private schooling plays a much greater role in secondary
education. Private schools account for 41 percent of enrollment in junior secondary education
and 54 percent in senior secondary. Junior secondary education is provided through 8,712 public
(46 percent) and 10,279 private (54 percent) general junior secondary schools (SMP). The
Ministry of Religious Affairs (MORA) administers the 582 public and 7,547 private madrasah
tsanawiyah muslim schools (MTs) at the junior secondary level. At the senior secondary level,
3,241 public and 10,380 private schools enroll a total of 4.2 million students, for a gross
enrollment rate of 34 percent.
1.12   When a private junior secondary school is established, it is "registered" and evaluated
after graduating its first students. Private schools can advance in accreditation from"registered"
to "recognized" and finally to the status of "equivalent" to public schools. Most equivalent
schools are owned and operated by religious organizations. Registered and recognized schools
are in general owned and operated by teachers' associations and other nonprofit organizations
(yayasans), although some are owned by religious organizations or small communities. Most
registered schools are owned and operated by small rural communities that do not have a public
school nearby. Almost invariably, schools established by communities aspire to complete
Government takeover, while private yayasans often prefer partial Government support so they
can maintain control. Schools established by teachers' associations often use public school
facilities after hours and employ public school teachers as instructors.
B.    SECTOR ISSUES AND NATIONAL INITIATIVES
1.13   Despite high gross enrollment rates, about 22 percent of primary school pupils, especially
in the more difficult remote areas, do not complete the full six years of education. Universal
nine-year education, however, depends on improving the efficiency of the primary system and
producing an expanded pool of candidates for junior secondary school. The Government has
started addressing this issue through a series of initiatives, including the Primary Education
Quality Improvement Project (Ln. 3448-lND) which is piloting measures for improving
education quality.
1.14  In spite of the constraints to access and quality at the junior secondary level, Indonesia
has made great strides in gender equality in both primary and junior secondary education. Only
slightly fewer girls than boys are enrolled in school at the national level. Girls also have lower



4                     Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
drop-out and repeater rates than boys and are often higher achievers. However, rural conditions
similar to those which present obstacles to the development of junior secondary education also
adversely affect the participation of girls. When schools are located a long way from homes,
parents are less willing to let their daughters commute long distances or to undertake the costly
expenditures of relocation to urban areas.
1.15  In addition to access and quality, all provinces face new and existing management
challenges as a result of the deconcentration of tasks from the center to the provinces, a process
this project will support. For historical and political reasons, public administration in Indonesia
has been highly centralized and decision making is tightly controlled by central Government in
Jakarta. The center has traditionally set objectives for education expansion and development,
with few inputs from the provinces. However, the differences of local conditions in each province
and district, accentuated by the great distances of the outer islands from the administrative center,
make it increasingly difficult for central administration to devise and implement satisfactory
solutions to local problems. Although Jakarta will retain a significant role in providing policy
guidelines and promoting regional equity and overall economic development, the Government has
decided gradually to devolve authority to the provinces and districts. This devolution will result in
a deconcentration of the functions of the MOEC to provincial (Kanwil) and district
(Kandep/Kanin) education offices.
Initiatives Related to Access
1.16   GOI is aware of these problems and a number of initiatives have been put in place to
address them. To improve access, the policy of establishing a minimum size of nine classrooms
for Government schools, which has prevented opening schools in small communities, is being
changed to make it easier for schools with fewer classrooms to be built.
1.17   Private schools supported by local communities, private educational foundations and
religious groups account for about 40 percent of total enrollment at the junior secondary level.
GO! supports these schools through a variety of measures, including provision of teachers, but
they are generally of lower quality and cost more to parents. The low quality in these schools is
explained by poor quality teachers and  scarcity of educational materials.  Unit costs of
operations in these schools is, however, lower than in public schools. Current and proposed GOI
support for private education would help maintain or increase its share of enrollment and
improve quality. In the more southern provinces of Sumatra (Jambi, South Sumatra and
Lampung), private schools are responsible for 30-50% of enrollment; in Aceh and West
Sumatra, however, only for about 17%.
1.18  In some sparsely populated areas, regular schools still will be far away for most students.
The SMP Terbuka ("open SMP") programs have been established to meet the needs of students
who cannot attend regular schools because of distance or high direct and opportunity costs.
These are non-fee-paying schools, and students attend afternoon classes one or more times a
week, usually at a junior secondary school, supplemented by tutorials in their villages.
1.19   To assist rural families who cannot afford to pay for education, GOI has established a
scholarship program for children of low economic background and satisfactory academic
achievement. This program covers all costs related to junior secondary education; within the



StaffAppraisal Report                               5
eligible group, priority is given to children from remote areas, orphans, and girls. GOI plans to
expand this program, to improve its effectiveness.
Initiatives Related to Quality
1.20   Various initiatives have also been introduced to improve the quality of junior secondary
education, including efforts to bring good teachers to underserved areas. Although there is an
excess number of teachers in the aggregate, there is an unequal distribution of teachers between
urban and rural areas within provinces because most rural teaching positions are considered
undesirable. To address this issue of shortages of (particularly core subject) teachers in rural and
remote areas, "contract teachers" are being proposed in rural schools in core academic areas.
Some rural communities have also provided their teachers with dormitories. In addition, the
Government is already providing some financial compensation for service in hardship areas.
Over the longer term, local recruitment is one practice that will help to keep teachers in rural
areas.
1.21   Developed from an initiative taken by the subject teacher quality improvement forum
(MGMP), an in-service teacher training program called PKG was introduced in 1983 to improve
the subject-matter knowledge and skills of teachers. This program was highly successful in
improving the pedagogical skills of teachers in the selected subject areas and created a positive
attitude among teachers where it was provided. There were however some serious shortcomings
of the program. The subject-matter knowledge of teachers was not fully addressed, development
of skills in identifying learning difficulties was neglected, and teachers in rural areas could not be
covered because of high costs and logistical difficulties. Training was provided every weekend
followed by classroom visits, and trainees were supposed to develop their own materials. Some
of the rural teachers who could be reached could not take full advantage of the program because
of weak qualifications. Revitalization of content and decentralization of the PKG and MGMP
system to the district level and the systematic involvement of regional MOEC staff, including
supervisors and principals in the training program, would help address this issue.
C.  JUNIOR SECONDARY EDUCATION IN THE SUMATRA PROVINCES
General Description of Project Provinces
1.22   The Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project covers five of the country's 27
provinces: (a) DI Aceh, (b) West Sumatra, (c) Jambi, (d) South Sumatra, and (e) Lampung.
Other recently approved projects are the East Java and NTT Junior Secondary Education Project
and the Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project. The project provinces face
challenges in the provision of junior secondary education that are common to all provinces in
Indonesia, though not to the same extent. These challenges are (a) to expand equitable access to
junior secondary education; (b) to improve the quality of education throughout the provinces;
and (c) to make education management more efficient as the provinces assume increased
responsibility. Access is restricted mainly by the lack of places; high direct and opportunity
costs; and great distances and limited transportation networks in some provinces. In some
instances, parents in very remote or difficult areas do not express interest in junior secondary
education because they think it is beyond their reach. The most important factors affecting



6                         Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
quality and internal efficiency are: teacher knowledge and skills; the availability of textbooks,
teaching materials, and equipment; the curriculum; pedagogical supervision; the flexibility of the
school calendar; school management; and examinations and educational assessment.

1.23    The five project provinces have populations of about three to seven million each,
accounting each for less than 4% of the total population in Indonesia, and less than 12% in the
aggregate. Except for Lampung, (at 181 people per km 2), population density is lower than in the
country nationally, with substantial remote and sparsely populated areas in all provinces.



Staff,Appraisal Report                               7
Population growth, however, over the last ten years is higher than for Indonesia as a whole, due
to higher than average fertility rates and in-migration from Java in most of these provinces
(except West Sumatra).
1.24 All five provinces remain predominately rural and agriculture still accounts for two-
thirds or more of total employment, while industry accounts for 18 to 20%, compared to 32%
nationally. Average GDP growth per year for the five provinces remains around the national
average, while GDP per capita (without oil) for 1993, however, in all provinces is below the
national average (from Rp. 803,000 for Lampung, to Rp. 1,302,000 for Aceh, against Rp.
1,436,000 nationally). With regard to the incidence of poverty, the provinces are similar to the
national average, while calorie and protein consumption are at the national average or better. As
is the case in the rest of the country, the poverty incidence has been declining.

8                     Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
1.25   A number of common constraints bear on further development of junior secondary
education in the five project provinces on Sumatra island, reflecting the geographic and
economic conditions of these provinces. With regard to primary and junior secondary education,
these provinces exhibit the following similar characteristics (See Table 1.2):
In primary schools:
*      A gross enrollment rate as high or higher than the national average.
*      A repetition rate higher than the national average and a lower than average
retention rate.
In junior secondary schools:
*      Gross enrollment rates lower than the national average for the three southern
provinces (Jambi, South Sumatra and Lampung), but higher for Aceh and West
Sumatra.
*      Similarly, lower transition rates from  primary schools for the same three
provinces vs. higher ones for the last two.
*      Wide differences between provinces in drop-out and repetition rates.
Generally, the education programs in these provinces face the following challenges:
(a)    All provinces have in varying degrees remote-access and sparsely populated
areas in mountainous regions, swamp areas and forested islands, making it
difficult to provide access widely through regular schools. Alternatives, such as
SMP Terbuka, are therefore appropriate.
(b)    The existence of substantial remote and rural areas has resulted in a particularly
severe problem of imbalance in teacher distribution, with rural schools lacking
teachers in all provinces.
(c)    The three southern provinces, Jambi, South Sumatra and Lampung, are subjected
to heavy and continuous in-migration from Java, both planned and spontaneous.
Education administrators need to be especially skilled in school expansion
planning under these conditions.
(d)    For junior secondary education, enrollments and other indicators are generally
below the national averages, although not by much, perhaps reflecting the more
difficult geographic conditions. These provinces, therefore, will need to make a
greater effort in reaching the targets.
DI ACEH
1.26   DI Aceh is located on the northern tip of the island of Sumatra and facing northern
Malaysia. From the perspective of the project, what distinguishes this province is:



StaffAppraisal Report                              9
*     In line with higher average household incomes and living standards, a better than
average education: some 36% of the labor force is educated at junior secondary
school level and above, compared with only 27% for the country as a whole.
*     As a result of higher family income and parental educational attainment, the
demand for junior secondary school is likely to remain high.
*     In primary education, Aceh is ahead of the country, with 99% of its primary
school age population enrolled in this cycle compared with 96% for the country
as a whole.
*     The primary-junior secondary school transition rate was over 78% compared
with 67% for the country as whole. However, due to the lower completion rate at
primary school, the junior secondary school enrollment rates were similar to
those of the country as a whole, at 59% for the gross enrollment rate, and 45%
for the net enrollment rate.
*     Access to junior secondary schools for the remote population living away from the
populated coastal areas is a problem.
WEST SUMATRA
1.27   West Sumatra is composed of three distinct regions, a volcanic highland, a long coastal
plain which is swampy in places and cultivated in others, and a string of forested islands lying
about 100 km offshore. Distinguishing characteristics of this province are:
*     Out-migration of 1.78 per thousand in 1980-90, with population density over the
1980-90 period increasing by less than the country average, and a population
growth rate, at 1.6% p.a. lower than the country average.
*     Some 33% of the labor force is educated at junior secondary school level and
above, compared with only 27% for the country as a whole. Similarly, illiteracy
rates are among the lowest in the country, at 8% versus 14% for Indonesia.
*     A diverse physical environment of the province and the presence of a large island
population resulting in uneven enrollment levels.
*     A high primary school repetition rate at 10%, compared with the country average
of 7.6%, perhaps due to children absenteeism in this mountainous province.
*     A primary-junior secondary school transition rate of 71% compared with 67%
nationally, with a high enrollment rate of 67% versus 58%.



10                    Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
JAMBI
1.28   The sparsely populated province of Jambi consists of a lowland basin of dense jungle,
peat bogs and swamps, drained by Sumatra's longest river, the Batang Hari, and its tributaries.
Distinguishing characteristics of this province are:
*      Vigorous in-migration (10.8 per thousand in 1980-90) with population density
increasing by 40% and high population growth rate.
*      Illiteracy rates are among the lowest in the country, at 7%  versus 14%  for
Indonesia.
*     Jambi's primary school net enrollment rate increased by about 8% in the 1991-92 to
1994-95 period, presumably because of the continuous arrival of migrant families
into the province, resulting in a significant improvement in the primary school
completion rate.
*      Low junior secondary education enrollment rates, due to the difficult terrain and
rapidly expanding population due to in-migration. (Note: Because of the large flow
of migrants into Jambi, educational statistics are difficult to interpret, particularly
levels and trends in enrollment rates.)
SOUTH SUMATRA
1.29   South Sumatra is the most populated of the five project provinces. Significant
characteristics from the project's perspective are:
*      Due to substantial in-migration from  Java (5.2 per thousand in 1980-90),
population density increased over the 1980-90 period by more than a third, and
population growth rate increased, at 3.1% p.a., much higher than the country
average of 2.0% in this period.
*     A lower-than-average performance in Primary Education on all indicators: Net
Enrollment Rate of 94.3, completion rate of 71.9, drop-out rate of 4.9 and
repetition rate of 9.1.
*     Junior secondary school gross enrollment rates lower than for Indonesia, at 52%
versus 58%.
LAMPUNG
1.30   Lampung occupies the southern most tip of Sumatra, separated from Java by the shallow
Sunda straits. From the project's perspective, distinguishing characteristics are:
*      A densely populated province similar in nature to Java, 181 persons per square
kilometer in 1990, three times the average for Sumatra, and twice the Indonesian
average of 93 persons per square kilometer nevertheless continuing to experience



Staff Appraisal Report                          11
in-migration from Java (1.8 per thousand in 1980-90), with population density
over the 1980-90 period increasing by over 30%.
*     Per capita income, at $400 p.a., is only 56% of the national average of $718 per
annum; average household expenditure levels were also 30% lower than the
country average, and did not rise in the 1990-1993 period compared with a 13%
rise for the country as whole.
*     Despite lower incomes and wages, average calorie and protein consumption was
somewhat higher than the country average, and then levels were maintained in
the 1990-1993 period. Consequently poverty incidence, at 11.6%, was lower than
the country average of 13.7% in 1993, and declined by 1.5% since 1990.
*     In line with lower income and the predominantly rural character of this province,
the educational level of the people of Lampung is lower than for the country as a
whole. Some 24% of the labor force was educated at junior school level and above
compared with 27% for Indonesia.
*     The province's primary school completion rate, at 62%, was 20% lower than the
national rate of 82% in 1994-95. Drop-out rates before 1991-92 were unusually
high at 16%-17%, affecting the output of the primary education system in the mid-
i990s. With drop-out rates down to about 3%, however, the completion rate is
likely to improve dramatically in the coming years.
*     The junior secondary school gross enrollment rates were lower than for Indonesia,
at 54% versus 58%.
The expansion of junior secondary education must address the relatively low
completion rate at the primary school level, which is 20% below the national
average. Although drop-out and repeater rates have fallen drastically, more may
need to be done to ensure that all primary school age children actually complete
this cycle.



12                   Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
2. GOVERNMENT AND BANK STRATEGY
AND LESSONS LEARNED
A.  GOVERNMENT STRATEGY
2.1    As part of the Repelita VI (1994/99) program, the Government set the important goal of
extending access to high quality junior secondary education. This objective is integral to the
goal of attaining universal coverage of nine-years of basic education by the end of Repelita VII
(2004/05). To support this goal, the Government has adopted several policy measures and
strategies: (a) it plans to provide additional resources to basic education; (b) it has abolished
junior secondary school fees; (c) it is providing free textbooks for all primary and junior
secondary schoolchildren; (d) it is supporting private junior secondary schools with public
teachers and other inputs to maintain and even increase their significant enrollment participation
at this school level; (e) it continues to upgrade teachers' skills for both public and private schools
through in-service programs; (f) it has started the construction or development of regular or
alternative schools in rural and remote villages; (g) it has phased out all diploma courses for the
preservice education of secondary school teachers (junior and senior) so all new teachers at that
level now need to have an SI (undergraduate degree); (h) it is upgrading existing junior
secondary school teachers to D-III (three-year post-secondary diploma); and (i) it is extending
subsidies (scholarships) to capable but poor students to begin or continue junior secondary
education.
B.  LESSONS LEARNED
2.2   Integration of the following lessons from previous Bank projects would help build on the
exceptional performance of the Bank in the education sector in Indonesia. All the 21 previous
projects have been judged satisfactory by OED, and they have set the stage for the more
challenging tasks in this sector. OED Document, No. 9752, Indonesian Education and the
World Bank: an Assessment of Two Decades of Lending, 1991: a) found that each of the
previous projects was successfully implemented; b) concluded that Bank support in education
projects was timely and gave valuable encouragement to the Government; and c) pointed out
that the projects were successes and achieved important goals, but also indicated shortcomings in
the quality of education and fragmented management and coordination --issues being addressed
by the present project.
2.3    OED Report. The 1991 report has several conclusions and recommendations that are
relevant for this project:
Issue: Institution building is basic to the long-term goal of increasing the self-sufficiency
of the Indonesian education system (p. 58).
Project response: Increased emphasis on institution building and self-management at
provincial and district levels.



Staff Appraisal Report                             13
Issue: Projects must be discussed within the framework of the policy dialogue (p. 65).
Project response: Regular discussions between MOEC and the Bank on development
priorities and the role of the Bank related to other donors are instrumental in determining
agenda for Bank lending.
Issue: Despite explicit goals, projects have not always achieved equitable distribution of
project benefits to the poor (p. 68).
Project response: Several project interventions are specifically targeted to the poor:
school construction in rural areas, SMP Terbuka and scholarship program.
Issue: Previous projects (mainly national in scope) were often overly complex or
underestimated absorption constraints and experience, resulting in unsatisfactory
implementation (p. 70).
Project response: The project has been designed with extensive participation from
communities, existing schools, district and provincial MOEC staff.
Issue: GOI should spend a greater amount of its budget on education, to achieve the
necessary quality and institutional objectives (p. 78).
Project response: Indonesia's education expenditures are 14 percent of total government
budget; The 1996/97 budget shows an increase of 20 percent over last year, of which 72
percent is spent on basic education (primary and junior secondary).
Issue: Despite its support from GOI and the Bank (p.79) role in providing educational
opportunities, the private system has received limited support from GOI and the Bank
(p. 79).
Project response: GOI is now committed to ensuring that the involvement of the private
sector in junior secondary education is maintained and strengthened. Recent projects have
helped private schools improve their quality.
2.4    Lessons from completed and ongoing projects in secondary education. Other lessons
have been drawn or confirmed by the General Operational Review of Secondary Education
Projects by HDD. Implementation of the Indonesia Secondary Education Project (Ln. 2472-
IND) and the ongoing Second Secondary Education and Management Project (Ln. 3158-lND) are
particularly relevant in this respect.
(a)    Overall Project Design. The completed and ongoing secondary education
projects have demonstrated that the expansion of secondary education should be
addressed through a dual strategy of increasing school places and improving the
quality of education. The proposed project would include both expansion and
quality improvement objectives, to ensure continuing demand for education and
to improve repetition and drop-out rates.
(b)    Flexibility in the Design of Components.  Attempts to impose standard
solutions to the variety of conditions in Indonesia (as in all large countries) have
not been successful, and GOI is working to correct this situation, the policy of
limiting the size of government schools to a minimum of nine classrooms has
been reviewed, to allow government schools to be established in communities



14                   Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
with small populations and experimentation has started with alternative smaller
models of junior secondary education.
(c)    Ownership and Participation. Local communities contribute significantly to
the development of education in their areas. Stakeholders and clients are also
well placed to evaluate project outcomes. They know when children are learning
and how investments in schools are being used. Stakeholder involvement (which
was emphasized during the preparation process) is critical to fully measure
project outcomes, to understand how a project is unfolding and to help develop
appropriate corrective measures when necessary. The project will promote and
supplement the use of local resources for education by providing assistance in
kind and incentives for both private and public education.
C.  BANK STRATEGY AND RATIONALE FOR INVOLVEMENT
2.5    This project, the third in a series of junior secondary education investments financed by
the Bank, supports the Bank's 1995 Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for Indonesia, which was
presented to the Board on March 21, 1995 and updated on June 4, 1996. The CAS endorses
Government's role towards establishing a regulatory framework and in supporting
decentralization and participation. In addition, the CAS highlights the importance of continued
emphasis on human resource development to promote economic growth, competitiveness, and
structural transformation, and on a more selective, efficient use of public resources. The design
of the proposed project is also consistent with the Bank's strategy of balanced assistance to the
education sector, which includes on-going and proposed assistance for (a) improving the quality
of primary education, including possible interventions in early childhood development; (b)
expansion and quality improvement of lower-secondary education; (c) quality improvement of
upper-secondary education; (d) selective interventions in higher education; and (e) an active
policy dialogue designed to redefine and refocus the Government's role in the education sector.
Before continuing with further projects in Basic Education in Indonesia, the Board and GOI will
hold a joint seminar, to discuss future strategy and Bank involvement.
2.6   The Bank's comparative advantage in supporting junior secondary education is based on
its long-standing and close working relationship with MOEC. The Bank has engaged in a
comprehensive dialogue with MOEC that reaches far beyond this project itself, since it has
helped to: (a) establish a solid conceptual and economic framework for the expansion and
improvement of junior secondary education and basic education in Indonesia over the next 20
years; (b) identify the comparative advantages for the Bank's and other donors' role in the
planned expansion and quality improvement process; and (c) strengthen the decentralizing trends
toward sub-central levels, province-specific projects, and local ownership. As a result of this
dialogue, the previous higlhly centralized, uniform approach to project design and implementation
has been abandoned and has been replaced by an approach that will rest on provincially based
projects. This approach recognizes the need to take into account specific provincial situations,
including economic growth trends and prospects, the existing education needs, labor market
conditions, private sector delivery capacity, levels of poverty, and geographic conditions. The
Bank has also been an active partner in the discussions on the priorities governing basic
education delivery, on the role of the private sector in secondary education and on the share of
Government spending that should be allocated to basic education.



Staff Appraisal Report                            15
2.7   The Bank is a trusted partner in basic education in Indonesia and has substantial
experience working with the Indonesian Government in this sector. Based on the Bank's
worldwide experience, GOI sought the Bank's advice on such issues as lower-cost alternatives
for junior secondary education, how to improve quality and how to tailor the proposed increased
access programs to local needs. The proposed project represents one in an overall program of
Bank support for expansion and quality improvements in basic education. Currently, the Bank is
starting two other secondary education projects (East Java/NTT Junior Secondary Education and
Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education) and the Government has requested that the Bank
provide both financial and technical assistance for future projects.  The junior secondary
education development program is supported by other donors, including ADB and OECF. This
project complements the assistance to basic education provided by several ongoing projects:
(a)    Second Secondary Education and Management Project. This project (Ln.
3158-IND), approved in 1990, supports: provision of quality inputs in secondary
schools (teacher training, equipment and books); review and redesign of the
student assessment system; and redesign of the information management system.
After initial problems, mainly due to the large national scope, the project was
restructured to operate in five pilot provinces, with satisfactory results. The
implementation experience from this project has provided the basis for the design
of the current project. In addition, the Bank is now very familiar with the issues
and the agencies involved in this subsector at the provincial level.
(b)    The Primary and Secondary School Teacher Development Projects. These
two projects (Ln. 3496-IND and 3979-IND) address pre-service training of
primary and secondary school teachers respectively.  They complement the
current project in the preparation and quality improvements of teachers and
substantial coordination between pre-service and in-service training is achieved
through the concurrent implementation of these projects. The Bank is also
heavily involved in ensuring that maximum coordination is achieved between
Teacher Development Projects and the in-service training components of the
Secondary Education Development Projects.
(c)    The Primary Education Quality Improvement Project. Approved in 1992,
this project (Ln. 3448-IND) provides quality inputs in primary schools in six
provinces. The issues related to primary schools that impact on transition,
enrollments and quality in junior secondary schools are being addressed in this
project on a limited scale. Project impact is now being reviewed, to determine
the further strategies for implementation on a larger scale.
(d)    The Book and Reading Development Project. (Ln. 3887-IND). The project
became effective in September 1995 and provides free textbooks to every
primary and junior secondary education student in all subjects. Books will be
provided both to public and private schools. The project provides one of the
most essential quality inputs into basic education on a systematic basis, and
complements the Secondary Education Development Projects, which will focus
on supporting non-textbook educational materials and teaching aids.



16                   Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
3. THE PROJECT
A.    PROJECT OBJECTIVES
3.1    The project would assist the MOEC in achieving a greater number of junior
secondary school graduates of better quality upon graduation. This would be pursued by:
(a)    expanding access to junior secondary education in a cost-effective and
equitable way;
(b)   improving the quality of junior secondary education; and
(c)   strengthening the education management capacity at all levels.
B.  PERFORMANCE INDICATORS AND CRITICAL SUCCESS FACTORS
3.2   Project impact will be assessed using the following indicators (see Annex 10):
(a)    achievement of project enrollment targets by province (satisfactory if 75
percent achieved) in new junior secondary schools constructed under the
project, by project completion date;
(b)   an increase in student examination scores for core subjects by at least 10
percent for each subject by the end of the project (on a score of I to 10, with
current averages between 3.5 and 6); and
(c)   implementation of at least 80 percent of the annual work program as
measured by the physical targets and budget spent each fiscal year for project
and routine activities in each province.
C.    PROJECT DESCRIPTION
Project Components
3.3   To meet the objectives of increasing access to junior secondary education, improving
the quality of junior secondary education, and enhancing education management, activities
related to the following components and subcomponents would be implemented selectively
and in varying degrees in the public and private systems of the five project provinces. The
total cost of the project is US$144.0 million, including contingencies.
3.4    Expand Access (estimated base costs of US$78.5 million). To improve access to
junior secondary education, the project would (a) create a total of 53,520 student school
places by building regular junior secondary schools with priority for underserved rural areas
where female participation is also lowest; (b) gradually expand alternative equivalency
programs by creating 27,600 places with greater quality inputs for those who cannot attend



StaffAppraisal Report                             17
regular schools, and monitor the programs' effectiveness; (c) student and teacher
accommodations for public schools in rural and remote areas; and (e) finance scholarships
for 32,600 talented poor students with priority given to girls in public and private schools.
See Annex 4 for more details on options for increasing access. During negotiations, the
Government gave assurances that it will (i) select locations for construction in accordance
with criteria agreed with the Bank and (ii) award scholarships in accordance with criteria
acceptable to the Bank (See para 6.01 [a]).
3.5    New schools will be built only in rural areas, where no existing schools are yet
available, either public or private. School location will be determined based on local
planning, which will take into account existing SMP schools and the locations of the feeder
SD schools. Six-classroom schools are preferred in these provinces, since once established
and filled, they are less costly to run than the smaller 3-classroom schools, which require a
larger number of teachers relative to the number of students.
3.6   Improve Quality (estimated base costs of US$35.2 million). To improve the quality
of junior secondary education, the project would (a) strengthen school teachers' subject-
matter knowledge and pedagogical skills, including identification of learning difficulties,
through inservice and upgrading training programs, and support measures to bring teachers
to rural areas; (b) enhance the learning environment by providing schools with tools for
teaching and learning, including educational equipment and materials; science teaching aids;
new teaching rooms; and library and reference books; and (c) provide contract teachers for
rural areas. All public schools, including schools supported by MOEC and by MORA, will
be included in the project. All private schools, subject to certain criteria, will also be eligible
for quality improvement inputs. Criteria for private school inclusion are all schools with: (a)
classroom size equal or greater than the provincial private school average; (b) own land and
school buildings; and (c) at least half of teachers as regular, full-time teachers. These access
and quality interventions would help increase access of girls in areas where gender
disparities still exist. During negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it will
employ contract teachers during the project, in the numbers and locations and with the
criteria as agreed with the Bank (See para 6.01 [a]).
3.7    Strengthen Education Management (estimated base costs of US$5.1 million). To
strengthen the management of the education system, the project would (a) train school
principals and other administrators in managing schools and providing pedagogical support
to teachers; and (b) develop managerial and data analysis capacity to allow provincial and
district staff to effectively handle their increasing responsibilities for education planning and
management. During negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it will carry out the
management training in accordance with criteria acceptable to the Bank (See para 6.01 [a]).
3.8    Project Management (estimated base costs of US$7.3 million). The project would
support the strengthening of institutional capacities as well as the operational costs of project
management by the Kanwils and Kandeps. Coordination requirements by the MOEC
Directorate of Secondary Education (Dikmenum) would be provided under the Central
Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project.



18                   Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
Detailed Description of Interventions for Each Province
DI ACEH
Access
3.9    New School Construction. (US$8.7 million equivalent, excluding contingencies).
During the project period, 39 new 6-classroom schools will be built, adding 9,360 student
places and representing 18% of the needs. Remaining requirements will be provided
through financing from other donors, establishment of private schools and direct government
financing (APBN).
3.10   Alternative Equivalency Programs.  (US$1.4 million equivalent, excluding
contingencies). As an alternative to the regular schools, support will be provided to establish
22 SMP Terbuka (open SMP), each enrolling about 200 students, for a total of 4,400
students. The project would provide the necessary books and equipment for each of the SMP
Terbuka; it would also provide motorcycles to allow teachers to visit the satellite sites served
by the SMP mother schools and which are often not easily reachable by public
transportation.
3.11   Construction of Teacher Houses and Student Dormitories. (US$1.1 million
equivalent, excluding contingencies).  In isolated areas, where it is difficult to attract
teachers or where most students live too far away from the school, dormitories will be
constructed. The project will support the construction of teacher housing for 23 remote
schools and 7 student dormitories during the five year project period.
3.12   Student Scholarships. (US$2.1 million equivalent, excluding contingencies). The
project would support an expansion of the existing Kanwil-managed scholarship scheme,
after evaluation of its impact and review of its implementation mechanisms. The scholarship
would provide Rp 25,000 per month (for 12 months and normally for three years) to selected
students. Eligibility criteria would focus principally on the family's income level and the
student's school performance. The scholarship would be offered to 5,300 SMP students in
Aceh.
Quality
3.13   Teacher  Training  and  Support.  (US$3.4  million  equivalent, excluding
contingencies). The project would finance formal and informal in-service training for SMP
teachers. Existing upgrading programs at the Open University and at the teacher training
institutes will be evaluated and new programs designed for those subjects still requiring
major upgrading support. Upgrading of existing teachers from their current degree (DI and
D2) to the required D3 degree will be supported for 639 teachers through the redesigned
teacher upgrading programs. An additional 1,003 teachers would be able to participate in
shorter upgrading certificate programs, to allow them to teach in a subject different from
their current one. For 9,300 teachers, the project would support the in-service MGMP
program during the project period, while PKG Model C training would be available for
teachers in remote areas if required (up to 10% of all teachers). Additional training for guru



StaffAppraisal Report                           19
inti (key teachers) will be supported, as well as school cluster meetings for 643 school
principals.
3.14   Learning Materials. (US$1.9 million equivalent, excluding contingencies). For all
existing 643 SMP schools in the province, both public and private, packages of library books
and educational teaching materials would be provided under the project. School principals
and teachers would be able to decide which items they would buy, and procurement will be
carried out for the province overall at Kanwil level.
3.15   Contract Teachers. (US$0.6 million equivalent, excluding contingencies). On a
pilot basis, the project would support the hiring of up to 166 contract teachers for rural
schools in core academic areas, such as Bahasa Indonesia, Mathematics, Science Education
and English, where teachers are often lacking. Criteria were established for the conditions of
the contract, which would be tied to a specific school and would be discontinued if the
teacher leaves that school or does not meet thle contractual requirements.
Management
3.16   Strengthening Education Management. (US$0.2 million equivalent, excluding
contingencies). GOI is deconcentrating education management, by giving Kanwils and
Kandep offices increased responsibilities over regional and local matters and in project
implementation.  The project would provide managerial and technical training to
administrative staff at central, provincial, district, subdistrict and school levels. Training
would cover 22 staff from the Kanwil and 130 staff from the Kandeps. At Kanwil and
Kandep level, the emphasis would be on planning (including micro-planning), policy
analysis, monitoring, and evaluation and education data analysis and dissemination. At sub-
district level, training would focus on education data management. At school level, training
would help principals of all public and private schools to enrich their skills in supporting
teachers and students, developing community participation and managing school facilities.
3.17  Information Management. (US$0.8 million equivalent, excluding contingencies).
The project would support the capacity of provincial and district education administration to
manage and efficiently use information, in light of the intended de-concentration of project
implementation.  Three information functions will be reviewed and  developed:  (a)
forecasting, planning and monitoring by Kanwil and Kandeps; (b) data processing by
Dikmenum and Kanwil; and (c) school education data management by Kandeps. The project
would provide technical assistance for the development of the functions, analytical and skills
training for staff, software and equipment.

guru inti (key teachers) will be supported, as well as school cluster meetings for 466 school
principals.
3.32   Learning Materials. (US$1.4 million equivalent, excluding contingencies). For all
existing 466 SMP schools in the province, both public and private, packages of library books
and educational teaching materials would be provided under the project. School principals
and teachers would be able to decide which items they would buy, and procurement will be
carried out for the province overall at Kanwil level.
3.33   Contract Teachers. (US$0.6 million equivalent, excluding contingencies). On a

schools in core academic areas, such as Bahasa lndonesia, Mathematics, Science Education
and English.
Management
3.34   Strengthening Education Management. (US$0.1 million, excluding contingencies).

analysis, monitoring, and evaluation and education data analysis and dissemination. At sub-
district level, training would focus on education data management. At school level, training
would help 932 principals and school administrators of all public and private schools to
enrich their skills in supporting teachers and students, developing community participation
and managing school facilities.
3.35  Information Management. (US$0.6 million equivalent, excluding contingencies).

manage and efficiently use information, in light of the intended de-concentration of project
implementation.  Three information functions will be reviewed and developed:  (a)

D.  PROJECT ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
3.54   The economic analysis conducted for the project consisted of. (a) analysis of
educational demand and the rationale for public investment in junior secondary education;
(b) fiscal analysis; (c) review of alternatives; and (d) cost-benefit analysis. Details are
provided in Annex 1.
3.55   There are both efficiency and equity grounds for additional public intervention in
junior secondary education in Indonesia today. It is primarily the poor and near-poor (and
mostly those in rural areas) who are not enrolling in school and whose participation declined
in the late 1980s to early 1990s. One of the primary factors explaining the low demand is the
high direct costs of education. A second factor is indirect (opportunity) costs which
exacerbated the effect of high direct costs. Coupled with stagnant real wages in the I 980s
and the effects of cuts in public expenditure on basic education in the mid-I 980s (school
construction came to a virtual halt), these factors contributed to faltering demand. The past
two years have seen the reversal of these trends as enrollment rates have risen significantly
in all provinces.
3.56   Since investment in junior secondary education is desirable on both efficiency and
equity grounds, the key questions for the Government are the budgetary implications of the
expansion and how to expand the system. The budgetary implications of the expansion are
sizable (8.7 billion; see Annex 1), but estimation shows that the policy is attainable if the
economy continues to grow at the rate attained in recent decades, additional funds are made
available to junior secondary including external borrowing, and the private sector retains its
current significant share.
3.57   Assuming 6%  per annum  real growth of the budget, the project's incremental
recurrent costs will take from 2 to 4% of the educational budgets, depending on the province,
to support investments representing 22.4% of total requirements (See Table 3.1).



StaffAppraisal Report                            27
Table 3.1: World Bank Involvement Relative to Total Junior Secondary Expansion.
Total              To Be Financed By             World
Requested    World   OECF   APBN   Private          Bank

3.58   The analysis of alternatives considered the role of the private sector in the expansion
program and the fiscal implications of mainly public expansions versus one which made
room for private expansion. The budgetary implications double in the second scenario (14.5
billion compared to 7.6 billion). There are additional merits to preserving the private sector.
First, given the current and projected surplus of teachers, a robust private sector is an
effective means for helping to clear the market for teachers. Second, Madrasah schools
(religious schools which currently enroll about 20 percent of junior secondary students) offer
access to junior secondary in small, poor communities and at a cost that is significantly lower
than even MOEC-supervised private schools. These schools also enroll a higher proportion
of girls than boys, reflecting the preference pattern of some families for non-secular
education. Third, maintaining the share of private schools at the junior secondary level
establishes the basis for sustaining the even larger share of private schools at the senior
secondary level (54 percent of enrollments in 1993/94).
3.59   Beyond the public/private alternatives, the Government has experimented with
alternative modes of delivering junior secondary education (in respect of the regular SMP
model). These include: SMP Kecil, SMP Terbuka and Paket B. While not entirely substitutes
for each other (or to the regular SMPs), and experimentation to improve their quality is still
ongoing, preliminary unit cost comparisons indicate that gains can be achieved by utilizing
these less costly alternative models ofjunior secondary education in rural and remote areas.
3.60   The economic rate of return for the project investment is estimated by province and
component (increasing access and enhancing quality, but excluding the management
component). The estimates are obtained by solving for the internal rate of return that equates
a stream of benefits and costs resulting from the investment over a thirty year horizon. The
benefits of the projects are derived from the increased numbers of students enrolled in junior
secondary in any given year, but also include the additional stream of students expected to
proceed to senior secondary and tertiary education. A monetary value of the benefits of each



28                   Sumatra Junior Secondary Educalion Project
stream of beneficiaries is estimated using the difference in wages between one level of
education and the next. Benefits will also accrue from investments in enhancing quality of
junior secondary. A benchmark assumption of a 15 percent additional wage premium earned
by a higher quality graduate is made. High returns were found for both quality and access
components, with returns on quality enhancements generally higher than for expansion of
access.
E.  PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND SUPERVISION
Project Management
3.61   Provincial Project Implementation  Units (PPIU). The project would  be
implemented by Provincial Project Implementation Units in each of provincial capitals
(Banda Aceh, Padang, Jambi, Palembang and Bandar Lampung), and would be overseen by a
Central Program Coordination Unit at MOEC's Directorate General for Secondary Education
(Dikmenum) in Jakarta (see Annex 8).  Each Kanwil would establish within their
administrative structure a Provincial Project Implementation Unit preferably headed by the
provincial Head of Division of Secondary Education (Kabid Dikmenum), assisted by a
Procurement Officer, a Disbursement Officer and a Teacher Development Officer. The
PPIU would be overseen by a Provincial Project Advisory Committee which would be
chaired by the head of MOEC provincial office (Kakanwil), and would include the provincial
directors of Secondary Education, Planning, Procurement, and Finance, representatives of
Dinas and Public Work, and a representative of the provincial office of MORA. At
negotiations, assurances were received from the Government that it would: (a) establish and
staff the five Provincial Project Implementation Units in accordance with terms of reference
acceptable to the Bank; and (b) establish the Central Project Advisory Committees with the
composition and terms of reference acceptable to the Bank, no later than March 1, 1997 and
maintain them until the completion of the project (See para 6.01 [b]and [c]).
3.62   The PPIU would be responsible for managing the implementation of various project
components through the services of Kanwil and Kandep sectors, and would be responsible
for preparing an annual implementation plan and budget to be submitted to the CPCU. The
PPIU would also be in charge of producing annual implementation performance reports. At
the Kandep level, implementation would be insured by a Task Force headed by the Kandep
Director (Kakandep) assisted by the head of planning section. Kandeps will be involved in
the following project activities:  (a) selection of contract teacher candidates; (b)
determination of school locations; (c) construction monitoring; (d) supervision of teacher
training activities; and (e) selection of scholarship candidates. Appropriate budget support to
carry out these activities will be made available to each Kandep.
3.63   Central Program Coordination Unit (CPCU). To support the junior secondary
education expansion program, Dikmenum will establish within its central structure a Central
Program Coordination Unit headed by the Director of Dikmenum, assisted by a National
Advisor, an International Advisor, a Procurement Officer, a Disbursement Officer, a
Monitoring and Evaluation Officer, and project-specific Liaison Officers for the different
Junior Secondary Education Projects. The CPCU will insure that the overall and annual



StaffAppraisal Report                           29
project implementation plans and budgets prepared by the PPIUs be in accordance with
project objectives. The CPCU will also supervise and provide broad technical guidance to
the PPIUs of all secondary education projects. At negotiations, Government assurances were
received that it will: (a) maintain the Central Program Coordination Unit in accordance with
terms of reference acceptable to the Bank; and (b) establish the Central Program Advisory
Committee with the composition and terms of reference acceptable to the Bank, no later than
March 1, 1997, and maintain them until the completion of the project.
3.64   The Central Program Advisory Committee would facilitate CPCU consultation and
coordination on major project implementation issues. It would include senior officials from
the MOEC, BAPPENAS, Ministry of Finance, Ministry of Religious Affairs, Ministry of
Home Affairs, State Ministry for Administration Reform (MENPAN), the State Civil Service
Commission (BAKN), and all five project Kakanwils.
3.65   Implementation Schedule. The proposed project would be implemented over a
period of five years, the completion date being March 31, 2002. The detailed schedules of
implementation of all project activities (Annex 12), costs by year and components (Annex
13) procurement (Annex 15), and disbursement (Annex 16) will serve as the basis for project
implementation and will be updated annually by the CPCU and the PPIUs. The closing date
of the project is September 30, 2002.
3.66   Technical Assistance. A total of 1,215 person/month will be required at a base cost
of US$4.8 million. In all project components, the technical assistance requirements would
be satisfied largely (90 percent) by national consultants. International consultants (10
percent) would be recruited only in areas in which local expertise is limited. International
expert assistance is designed as institution building particularly at the province level rather
than "substitutive" services directly rendered as part of project implementation activities (see
Annex 14).
3.67   Project Launch Workshop. A project launch workshop will be conducted to
facilitate project start-up activities, familiarize PPIU and CPCU staff and Kanwil project-
associated staff with project and Bank procedures--especially for procurement--and
guarantee thereby adequate and timely project implementation. This workshop has a crucial
importance for project implementation due to the province-based management structure and
the incipient experience with project-related Bank requirements at province level.
3.68   Project Implementation  Plan.   For the  day-by-day  practice  of project
implementation CPCU, PPIU and Kandep staff will rely upon the Project Implementation
Plan. The PIP will describe in detail and in concrete terms the methodology, procedures and
multiple steps of project implementation and project monitoring. The PIP is expected to: (a)
become the key and uniform point of reference for all levels of project management; (b)
provide users with ready answer for problem solving; and (c) offset the relatively insufficient
lack of experience with project management at the provincial level.



30                   Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
Project Supervision
3.69   Monitoring and Evaluation. Monitoring of project implementation will be the
responsibility of the PPIUs at the provincial level, and of the CPCU at the central level,
which has a Monitoring and Evaluation Officer. The implementation progress of the project
will be assessed through monitoring activities and formative evaluation using the
performance indicators as indicated in para 3.2 and Annex 10, and will be carried out by an
independent agency or party. The above indicators will also form the basis of technical
supervision by the Bank. The supervision plan is summarized in Annex 11. Data from the
detailed performance indicators for monitoring and evaluation (Annex 10) would be
incorporated in the annual progress reports prepared by the Government and submitted to the
Bank. At negotiations, Government assurances were received that it would implement
policies and procedures to enable adequate project moniitorinig and evaluation in accordance
with agreed indicators (See para 6.01 [e]).
3.70   Annual Implementation Reviews. Reviews would take place each project year to:
(a) analyze the previous year's performance and budget execution against project objectives
and indicators; (b) analyze the proposed work plan and budget for the following year; (c)
discuss adjustments to be made in the project's implementation schedule; and (d) analyze the
results of the project evaluation. The proposed work plan and budget for the following year
would have to be previously defined at the central and provincial levels and contain a
detailed description of targets and planned activities. Plans for civil works would specify
type and schedule of construction and provide evidence of ownership or donation of school
or dormitory site and of adequate assignment of school teachers and staff. At negotiations,
Government assurances were received that it will: (a) prepare and present to the Bank annual
reports on budgets for the next year, by October 31 of each year, starting in 1997; and (b)
prepare and present to the Bank annual progress reports by June 30, starting 1998, of project
implementation of the previous fiscal year (See para 6.01 [g], and [h]).
3.71   Mid-term  Review.  A comprehensive mid-term review would be conducted to
assess project performance and initial impact in light of project objectives and expressed in
project indicators and Government policies in addition to review activities normally covered
under annual implementation reviews. The mid-term review would have a strong focus on
the formulation and carrying out of sectoral policies, particularly for institutional
strengthening at both province and district levels, and on redefining the component or
element where major problems were positively identified. The PPIU and the CPCU would
be responsible for hiring consultants and preparing the final terms of reference for the mid-
term review, based on experience accumulated under the project and the final or preliminary
results of studies, satisfactory to the Bank, and for preparing the report with
recommendations. At negotiations, Government assurances were received that it will
prepare and present to the Bank a mid-term report on project implementation and plans for
the remaining years of the project, by June 30, 1999; and review the report together with the
Bank by October 31, 1999. (See para 6.01 [i]).
3.72   Technical Audits. Dikmenum has the overall responisibility to monitor all project
activities and routine monitoring would be done through annual reviews. However, because
a large number of contracts would be financed under SOE procedures, an independent,



StaffAppraisal Report                            31
qualified firm with external technical assistance would be hired to conduct technical audits
of key project activities. The audits would cover activities in progress and post reviews of
completed actions to examine whether implementation of the various project components are
technically appropriate and to recommend corrective measures if necessary. At negotiations,
Government assurances were received that technical audits would be carried out every year
during the implementation of the project in accordance with terms of reference and in a
manner acceptable to the Bank, and that copies of the technical audit reports will be
furnished to the Bank for comments no later than three months after the end of each Fiscal
Year (See para 6.01 [f]).
3.73   Completion Report. A project implementation completion report is due no later
than six months after the Closing Date of the loan or such later date as may be agreed for this
purpose between the Borrower and the Bank. The report to be compiled by MOEC is
expected to provide a critical overview of project inputs, processes and outcomes. More
precisely the completion report will summarize (a) project results and impact on the basis of
indicators; (b) key project activities and management; (c) institutional lessons learned from
project implementation; (d) the project's financial status; (e) implementation issues and
recommendations; and (f) the plan designed to ensure the continued achievement of the
objectives of the project. At negotiations, Government assurances were received that it will
furnish to the Bank within six months after the project closing date a plan designed to ensure
the continued effectiveness of the project (See para 6.01 U]).



32                 Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
4. PROJECT COSTS, FINANCING, PROCUREMENT AND
DISBURSEMENTS
A.    PROJECT COSTS
4.1    Summary of Costs. The total cost of the project is estimated at US$144.0 million
equivalent (Rp 335.7 billion), including contingencies and identifiable taxes and duties. Tables
4.1 a, 4.1 b, 4.2a and 4.2b summarize the estimated costs by project component, category of
expenditure and by implementing agency, respectively. Detailed project costs are given in
Annex 13.
4.2    Bases of Cost Estimates. Baseline costs are estimated at June 1996 prices. Costs of
construction of school facilities are based on the GOI standard unit costsa per square meter
for such facilities and on review of costs of comparable facilities under other projects.
Equipment costs are based on recent costs available from other education projects. Costs of
educational materials are based on prices currently being paid for similar items under ongoing
education projects. Costs of domestic and international consultants are in line with recent
education and training projects for appropriately qualified experts. Costs of in-country training
(in-service teacher training, management training, seminars and workshops) are derived from
experiences under the ongoing secondary education and management project and vary widely
depending on the type of training being given and its duration and location, and they are in line
with the government guidelines. Scholarship grants are about US$130 per scholarship-year.
Operational costs are based on standard government allowances for honoraria, travel, per diem
and consumable materials.
4.3    Contingency Allowances. The project consists of a large number of items of small
monetary value. As the exact number of these items is not vital to the success of the project, no
physical contingencies have been included. A contingency allowance of US$17.9 million
equivalent represents 14 percent of the baseline costs for estimated price increases. Price
contingencies have been calculated at 8.7 percent per year for local costs and at 2.4 percent for
foreign costs.
4.4    Foreign Exchange Costs. Foreign exchange costs are estimated at US$28.4 million,
representing about 20 percent of total project costs, and are based on a detailed analysis of
expenditures under similar projects in Indonesia. The foreign exchange percentages for the
major expenditure categories are estimated as follows: (a) civil works and furniture - 30
percent; (b) equipment - 70 percent; (c) international consultants - 80 percent; (d) in-country
training, and educational materials - 15 percent; and (e) domestic consultants and operating
costs - 10 percent.
a GOI issues and updates annually the standard construction unit costs per square meter for every district.



Staff Appraisal Report                                      33
4.5     Taxes and Duties. Taxes and duties are estimated at Rp 14.3 billion (US$6.2 million
equivalent) based on the current 10 percent value added tax on all contracts for civil works,
equipment and other goods and tax exemption of educational books. This would be financed
fully by the Government of Indonesia.
4.6     Recurrent Costs.  Under the proposed project additional recurrent costs would be
generated by the recruitment of additional teachers, maintenance of new school facilities and
running of the new schools. The annual additional recurrent costs generated by these items are
estimated from Rp 1.8 billion (for Jambi) to Rp 6.0 billion (for Lampung) at January 1996
prices, for the different provinces, after the project is completed. These represent between 2%
and 4% of the education budgets (see Annex 2 for details). Thus, the long-term recurrent cost
implications of the project in all provinces are small and manageable.

taxes (99 percent of foreign and 61 percent of local expenditures). The Government would
finance the remaining costs of US$46.0 million equivalent including taxes (32 percent of total
project costs including taxes), as detailed in Table 4.3 below. The provincial budget allocation
would be done through annual provincial-level development budget.
