INDONESIA
Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
TEACHER TRAINING AND SUPPORT
A.    Background
l.    Over the past twenty years the Indonesian government has provided two major kinds of
teacher education activities for its junior secondary school (SMP) teachers, pre-service education
and in-service education. In the 1 970s and 1 980s, when access to secondary education was
expanding very rapidly, there were chronic shortages of secondary school teachers. Thus, various
diploma programs were created to provide post-secondary pre-service teacher education
opportunities for new teachers. For many years the government operated a special one-year training
program (PGSMTP) for SMP teachers which provided D-1 certification; gradually preparation
shifted to IKIPs and FKIPs which offered two year D-ll programs. By the end of the 1980s, as
supply began to catch up with demand, all PGSMTP programs had been closed and D-11 programs
were being phased out. By the early 1990s, all secondary school teachers, SMA and SMP, were
expected to earn an SI university degree. At the same time, new elementary school teachers were
required to obtain D-II certification and practicing teachers assisted in upgrading to the D-ll level.
Under these conditions, it was considered necessary to provide D-III upgrading for SMP teachers.
This has been a large task since a majority of teachers still have D-1 or D-I1 qualifications. (The
proportion with below D-1I1 qualifications as of 1994/95 varied from 55% in West Sumatra to 95%
in Jambi.) D-III upgrading is now well underway in Science, Math, and English; it has recently
begun in Bahasa Indonesia; it has yet to begin in Social Studies.
2.    Also, as a consequence of the policy requiring 9 years of basic education (SD + SMP), more
SMPs are being opened in rural areas. Given the size of the catchment area in such areas, many of
these SMPs are quite small, having perhaps one or two classes per grade. In such circumstances it is
difficult to justify a full complement of teachers covering all subject areas. This means that many
teachers have to teach in more than one subject area. Under the recently launched, Bank-supported
Secondary School Teacher Education project many new SMP teachers will be prepared during pre-
service teacher education to teach more than one subject. For those who have already been pressed
into such service, special teacher certification programs are needed to help them become better
prepared to teach subjects in which they have not been trained.
3.    With respect to inservice of education, the country has succeeded over the past twenty years
in developing an extensive group of secondary school teachers into teacher trainers both at the
provincial and district levels. At the provincial level, instructors have been trained, many at the
advanced graduate level (MA) to plan school-based teacher education efforts (originally called
Pemantapan Kerja Guru or PGK, now usually referred to us Musyawara Guru Mata Pelajaran or
MGMP), to train the local tutors for those programs (Guru Inti), and to manage special PKG
programs (Model C) for teachers from remote area schools. Guru Inti (key teachers) have been
trained and commissioned to provide in-service activities at the district and sometimes school cluster
level. Whereas this program has provided invaluable human resources for school-based quality
improvement activities, in recent years the program has become routinized and therefore less



Annex 6: Page 2 of 4                             81
effective than original pilot activities. Now evidence of improved pedagogy is hard to find outside
the most favored schools in large urban centers. It can be said that Indonesian junior secondary
educators deliver a body of planned curricular content, but pay insufficient attention to what and
how pupils are learning. Thus, in order to improve teachers' classroom effectiveness, changes in
school-based teacher development activities are suggested in three areas:  curriculum  content,
delivery mechanisms and training program management. The recommendations for the reform of
the in-service teacher training component of the project are based on findings of the JGF-sponsored
report, "Secondary Education in Indonesia:  Strengthening Teacher Competency and Student
Learning."
B.    Project Activities
4.    The teacher training component of the current project will consist of three kinds of activities
for SMP teachers: a) strengthening and supporting teacher D-111 teacher upgrading activities; b)
supporting second-subject teacher certification activities; and c) reforming and supporting the
delivery of school-based teacher development activities.
5.    D-III Upgrading. This project element will consist of three sequenced stages:
first, a careful evaluation of past upgrading activities, focusing on the relevance of the curriculum
and training to teacher knowledge-development needs and the impact of programs on teacher and
student classroom performance; second, redesign of the upgrading program, including curriculum,
delivery systems, and evaluation methods, based on the evaluation results, and third, the
implementation of the D-III upgrading for up to 3900 teachers in the project's five Sumatra
provinces in the field of IPS and Bahasa Indonesia.
6.    Second-Subject Teacher Certification. This project element will provide teachers with the
opportunity for second-subject certification. Certification activities, managed either by the Open
Learning University (for distance education) or local teacher training Institutes or Faculties (lecture
mode), will cover 18-20 credit hours and will last about one year (two semesters). The program
focuses on the subject matter contained in the SMP curriculum (revised 1994). Participants are
expected to study in fields related to that of their original certification: science majors can certify in
math and vice-versa; history in geography and vice-versa, and Indonesia majors in English and vice-
versa.
7.    Although there are many teachers in other fields who are underutilized in small SMPs (e.g.,
art, music, skills, health and PE), this project will only cover those teachers who are known to be
teaching in two or more of the above interrelated fields. This project will thus begin by the
Kabupaten's drawing up a list of all teachers who qualify, complete with the subject areas in which
candidates need second-subject certification. This will be submitted to Kanwil which will develop a
plan for this activity, using either local instructions or the Open Learning University system,
depending on local circumstances.
8.    School-Based Teacher Development Activities. This project element will consist of two
distinct types of activities:  first, reforming the content of existing teacher in-service training
programs, and second, providing support for the reformed delivery and management of such teacher
training.



82                       Annex 6: Page 3 of 4
Training program reform. The content of all in-servicing activities are expected to be
revitalized, and as a result, future programs will pay much more attention to promoting
student learning than they do at present. Emphasis will be given to the development of
effective presenting, explaining, questioning, and probing techniques, so that teachers
become skilled in identifying and addressing students' learning strategies and difficulties.
Teachers will learn to construct open-ended formative tests for use in the classroom, and to
use the results to improve learning. The reform activity will be spearheaded under the East
Java and NTT Junior Secondary Education Project and its results will be applied to this
project.
Training Program Delivery and Management. There will be two basic delivery mechanisms
in the project for on-going teacher in-service education. The first is for teachers who work in
remote area schools, those for which travel to the nearest neighbor school takes a day or
more. This mechanism is currently referred to as PKG, Model C. Teachers from these
schools will receive in-service education having the following features:
* participating teachers (those teaching the 6 core curricular subjects) will receive block in-
service education at the province level managed by instructors. There will be three blocks in
a year before the beginning of trimester three (one week). In-service blocks will emphasize
the development of teacher skills in promoting student learning as mentioned in the
"program reform" section above; this they will do through discussion of essential subject
matter concepts and student misconceptions, observation of mastery lessons, and live
teaching opportunities.
* receiving "on-service" visits from the instructor at least three times a year, after every in-
service block. During these on-service visits, teachers will be observed teaching a lesson in
which they try to incorporate ideas covered during their in-service blocks; receive feedback
and guidance from the instructor; observe a model lesson presented by the instructor, if one
is considered useful; and discuss teaching progress and problems with the instructors,
together the school who are not currently in the in-service cycle.)
9.     The second mechanism  of in-service education is that provided within the context of a
cluster of schools, a new variation of the system now being called Musyawara Guru Mata Pelajaran
(MGMP), designed especially for schools in cities and easily accessible rural areas. This mechanism
will be implemented at the district level and will operate through clusters of neighboring schools
(five to eight, including private schools and madrasah tsanawiyah). Teachers will gather at the
cluster center for in-service activities at least five times a trimester under the leadership of the guru
inti, following which teachers will each receive on-service visits from the guru inti. These activities
will be essentially independent and self-financing, but will be eligible for Project support under
certain conditions (criteria to be defined in the implementation plan). More detailed features of the
system and its management are as follows:
*Management. The program will be managed by Ka Kandep or his/her representative,
working with group head teachers, who themselves represent the heads of their cluster of
schools. The management team will divide the district into clusters of appropriate size, and



Annex 6.: Page 4 of 4                              83
will be responsible for finances (particularly yearly requests for Project funding), the
maintenance of records, and arranging the guru inti's work schedules.
*Guru inti. Several sets of guru inti have already been trained at the district level. Given the
organization of school clusters as the site for cluster-based training, several more sets of guru
inti will need to be trained under the project (an average of 160 per province; training to be
at PPPGs in Java during two consecutive years). Two inodels of guru inti deployment are
possible (preferences to be refined during implementation planning): a) several sets of guru
inti will be selected from district schools -- but not necessarily all clusters -- and deployed in
cluster training and visits according to a district level deployment plan; b) one set will be
selected and trained from each school cluster and deployed in that cluster. In either    case,
official arrangements will need to be made (SKs) to maintain the guru inti's teaching load at
their own school at a reduced level (an average of 12 hours per week).
*Meetings. Cluster level teachers meetings will be held on a regular basis, approximately 15
times per year.  A cluster level management team  consisting of the head teachers of
theparticipating schools will set the timetable, but the guri inti will be responsible for
organizing and running the all-day meetings.
*School visits. Guru inti will carry out systematic visits to all junior secondary schools in the
cluster, public and private (if they are receptive), on a regular basis. During the visits, the
guru inti will observe a lesson taught by every teacher in her/his specialty area, provide
feedback and write a report highlighting achievements as well as identifying areas where
there is need for improvement. A file of reports will be maintained.
*Visits for the Provincial Team. Subject specialist pengawas (supervisors) and instructors
will visit each district on a regular basis to guide and monitor the guru inti 's work. They will
review the guru inti's work. They will review the guru inti's school reports and visit cluster
meetings.
*Incentives for improvement will be introduced including the display of outstanding
students' work at the cluster center, the presentation of model lessons by outstanding
teachers at special cluster meetings, and opportunities for upgrading (to SI) and promotion
for guru inti.
*Funding. The project will provide funding for guru inti training, travel to and preparation
for cluster meetings, travel for school visits. It will also contribute a small amount (Rp
25,000 per cluster) for materials and supplies. Teacher travel expenses and consumables
(e.g. meals and snacks) should be covered by their own schools (swadana) unless the
school's discretionary funds (BP3) are so small and/or travel is so expensive that the school
cannot cover them. In this case, the school is eligible to apply for project support to cover its
teachers' participation in cluster activities (up to Rp 1 0,000 per teacher per cluster meeting).



84                     Annex 7: Page I of 4
INDONESIA
Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
INFORMATION MANAGEMENT AND UTILIZATION COMPONENT
1.    Goals and Objectives of the Information Management and Utilization
Component
The overall goal of the IMUC is to have MOEC managers and staff making effective
management decisions and resource allocations based on timely, useful and accurate
information. To reach this goal, the IMUC proposes a strategy for improving information
management and utilization by MOEC managers and staff.
The basic approach is to make data available to user groups, provide them with tools
to analyze the data, train them to understand what the information means, and cultivate an
appreciation of information based decision making. This approach will focus on three
ingredients necessary for an effective JSEP-MIS: tools, skills and appreciation.
Each of the four information components have specific objectives:
FPM-Forecasting Planning and Monitoring: Kandep offices are able to produce
annual plans and monitor inputs, processes and outcomes throughout the year. This,
in turn, will lead to better planning, efficient allocation of resources and solution of
management problems.
SEMINS-Secondary Education Management Information System: Kandep,
Kanwil and PUSAT information staff are able to maintain data warehouses
(consisting of existing data sets) and use reporting tools to support decision making.
Similar to the FPM, managers will be able to analyze trends, identify problems and
follow up. Information and analytical tools are now placed closer to the providers
and immediate supervisors (i.e. Kandep offices and schools).
STOKK-School To Kandep and Kanwil information: School are better able to
manage and plan their resources, and Kandep offices provide direct support to
schools. Schools send quarterly data to Kandep offices (four indicators). Kandep
directly collect and process, and use the information produced for management
purposes (reduce dropouts, improve Ebtanes scores, reduce teacher classroom
absenteeism, etc.). Feedback reports are periodically sent to schools and followed
up.  As a result, the school management role of Kandep offices is significantly
increased, and they play a more active and supportive role.
Informatic Support: Hardware, software, skills and organization are available in
PUSAT, Kanwil and Kandep offices to support the management of information and
decision making. PUSAT and Kanwil offices will now have the tools, resources and
know-how to support their own managers, as well as Kandep offices and schools.



Annex 7: Page 2 of 4                       85
2.    Methodology and Subcomponent Description
The FPM is a forecasting, planning and monitoring tool to used for managing and
planning the JSEP. The SEMINS provides new information tools and skills to better use
existing data for decision making. The STOKK component is design to assist Kandep and
Kanwil offices to better support schools through the periodic collection and analysis of
school data. The informatic support component provides the tools, skills and other inputs
required by the FPM, SEMINS and STOKK.
(a) FPM: Forecasting, Planning and Monitoring Tool. The objective is to provide
reliable information on educational resource needs, develop plans based on needs and
available resources, monitor and manage the implementation process, and annually re-
plan to reach targets and utilize resources efficiently and effectively. Computerized
applications and manual forms will be developed and used for forecasting, planning and
monitoring. Technical guides will also be developed, and staff trained. The FPM is
developed largely for the JSEP and will be used to improve the current MOEC planning
process by providing a comprehensive analysis of past results, and explicit projections of
future objectives and required inputs.
The FPM is an information tool for translating SMP needs and available resources into
Kandep/Kanwil plans, monitoring the execution of JSEP results and activities, and then
reviewing the results and annually.  A cyclical planning-management approach is
proposed that forecasts needs, develops a plan (activities, time and budget), monitors and
manages the implementation process, and reviews and re-plans annually.
(b) SEMINS: Secondary Education Management Information System for Improved
Management Decision Making. The objective of SEMINS is to improve management
decision making and resource allocation through 1) providing timely/reliable information
in accessible formats, 2) improving data management, utilization skills and appreciation,
and 3) providing requisite hardware/software and support systems to insure data
processing and information production. The problem facing the JSEP is there are too
much data but limited information, analysis and utilization.   The SEMINS  aims to
better use existing data sets to monitor key indicators, identify problem areas and
implement solutions. The strategy is to provide information management tools, skills,
improved internal and external data sets, and management appreciation for informed
decision making. Information processing and analysis tools will be provided, and staff
will be trained how to use the tools and the information produced. The SEMINS will use
database applications and GIS to produce information in tabular, graphic and map
formats.
The SEMINS approach is to provide data analysis tools to managers who have been
trained to collect/process key data, identify problems and achievements, and utilize the
value of information for routine management and planning. The SEMINS will begin
with the formulation of a team, a systematic audit of existing information, selection of
key indicators to be viewed over time and cross-sectionally, followed by the design or
application of computer tools such GIS or interactive query programs.



86                     Annex 7: Page 3 of 4
(c) STOKK: Collection and Use of School Data in the Kecamaten, and Kandep and
Kanwil DIKMENUM Offices. The STOKK data management component will support
primarily Kandep and Kanwil offices by focusing on data from school and the direct
supervision and support by Kandep staff. Data from schools go directly to Kanwil and/or
PUSAT offices or are simply passed through Kandep offices with little processing,
analysis and feedback to schools. As a result, Kandep offices are often viewed as passive
conduits for data going to Kanwil and PUSAT offices.  Moreover, little feedback
information is received from higher level offices and virtually none is fed back to schools
(the basic information providers).  The STOKK approach proposes to address this
problem by providing information tools, skills and new roles to Kandep level staff.
The objective of the STOKK is to improve the direct management of schools by Kandep
offices (and the internal management of schools themselves) by providing Kandep
offices with the means to identify key problems areas and provide remedial action or
support.
The STOKK approach proposes to collect quarterly data from schools, compile the data
and analyze at the Kandep level, then feedback results to schools accompanied with
support and other remedial action. The content is very limited and focuses only on key
indicators: I ) quarterly Ebtanas scores. 2) teacher availability by subject area, 3)
coverage of classroom sessions by teachers, 4) number of students and dropouts, and 5)
availability of local school funds. The initial collection and scanning of Ebtanes sheets
would still be carried out in Kanwil offices (were OMRs)l but the results will be
immediately fed back to Kanwil offices and included on a Kanwil database showing
average scores for schools and trends. Items 2, 3, 4 and 5 will be collected on a small
form and transferred to Kandep offices directly. Kandep offices will be provided with
data management tools and skills to compile and use the information for routine
management and planning.
(d)    Informatics  Support  (hardware, softwvare, organization  and  skills).
Improvements in the information systems of' PUSAT, Kanwil and Kandep offices are
necessary to support the FPM, SEMINS and STOKK componenits. The following is a
description of the information improvement areas. These include 1) the development of
LANs in Kanwil offices that link data sets and users, 2) the maintenance of data bases in
PUSAT, Kanwil and Kandep level offices that allow access to key indicators by time, 3)
the provision of stand-alone computers to Kandep offices, 4) skill development and
guidelines, and 5) technical assistance to develop these systems. These improvements
will require the following informatic inputs: procurement and maintenance of hardware
(computers and peripheral equipment), software applications (off-the-self and custom
applications), and data sets (internal and external).
Related with the three core components is an effort to increase the appreciation of
managers regarding the utility and value of informationi. This will require improving the
accuracy and relevance of data for short and long-range decision making. Moreover
managers will require that information can be produced in formats that can be understand
and address salient questions. As a result, the plan for Informatic Support stresses the
need to develop informatic skills and organization at the PUSAT and Kanwil levels
Funding for the OMR readers to process Ebtanes scoresheets is not included in this budget.



Annex 7. Page 4 of 4                         87
which can, in turn, support Kandep staff and school head masters utilize information
more effectively.
LAN: procurement, allocation and deployment of Kanwil LAN: Kanwil LAN
systems that network various bidang and support data processing and reporting tools
will permit the sharing of data and improve access. The objective is to support a
Kanwil data warehouse (with data from schools, Kandep offices, Kanwil bidang and
the PUSAT office) that can be accessed by any Kanwil level bidang. A central
Information Unit (IU) should be established (with client workstations) to maintain
the LAN while cables would link all of the key bidang offices. Several Kanwil
offices already have a LAN and trained support staff that could serve as a model, as
well as the central DIKMENUM office.
Client Server Database Systems. Data warehouses of existing data, plus FPM and
STOKK, will require client server hardware and software support at the PUSAT and
Kanwil levels. Only stand alone (un-networked) PC's will be used in Kanwil offices.
Client server database software will be installed on the PUSAT and Kanwil level
LAN systems. Desktop versions of the same (or reduced) databases will be installed
on Kanwil computers.
Desktop "Stand-alone" Systems. Stand-alone computer systems will only be
installed in Kandep offices that have met certain criteria and are certified. Section
3.4.3 describes the hardware, software, training and technical assistance
requirements. Since Kandep offices will not receive computers until established
criteria are met, installation will be phased in over the first 1-2 years of the JSEP,
thus allowing all Kandep offices time for meeting the criteria and training of staff.
By the third year of the project, all Kandep offices are expected to have trained staff
and 3 operational computers.
Technical Assistance for Designing and Developing the 3 subcomponents. In each
of the four main information system components, technical assistance will be
provided by technical advisors, MOEC teams, Information Unit (largely for training
and maintenance support) staff, and Local Computer Groups.



88                      Annex 8: Page I of 6
INDONESIA
Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
PROJECT MANAGEMENT ORGANIZATION STRUCTURE
Terms of Reference
Central Program Advisory Committee
Objective. To provide policy guidance to the Central Program Coordination Unit from
the perspective of education authorities both in the Directorate of Secondary Education and in
other relevant agencies and in MOEC and other ministries, and to provide a forum for
discussing education policy and planning issues which will have an impact on project
implementation.
Members. Chaired by the Director-General of Primary and Secondary Education, the
committee will comprise the following members:
(a) MOEC:
Secretary General
Director-General, Higher Education
Head, Research and Development Office
(b) Other Agencies:
Head, Bureau of Religion, Education, Culture and Sport, BAPPENAS
Director-General, Binbaga, MORA
Director-General, Budget, Ministry of Finance
Tasks. The tasks of the Central Program Advisory Committee will consist of:
(a)    Annual coordination meetings to review project progress and discuss major
policy issues affecting project implementation;
(b)    Annual review of provincial annual work plans and budgets;
(c)    Exchange information on medium and long-term education policies and plans.
Terms of Reference
Provincial Project Advisory Committee
Objective. The objective of the Provincial Project Advisory Committee is to ensure
that project implementation will be consistent with project objectives and Government policy.
The Committee will provide overall implementation guidance to the PPIU.



Annex 8: Page 2 of 6                          89
Composition. The Provincial Project Advisory Committee will be chaired by
KaKanwil and its membership will be comprised of related agencies and institutions dealing
with the education system.
Tasks. The advisory committee will give guidance to project implementors on the
merits of proposals submitted. The committee will review proposals from kabupaten and
kecamatan to make sure that annual work plans are in line with implementation criteria and
guidelines. This committee will work closely with the existing provincial Coordination Team
for Nine Years Basic Education (Tim Koordinasi Wajar). The advisory committee will meet at
least twice a year, or whenever major project decisions need to be made.
Terms of Reference
Central Program Coordination Unit
Objectives. The Central Program Coordination Unit (CPCU) will have three
objectives: (a) it will monitor overall progress, and ensure that project implementation (as it
unfolds through the Annual Work Plan and Budget process) is in accordance with project
objectives and agreed project criteria, rules and guidelines; (b) it will guide the provinces and
provide (or locate the sources of) the necessary technical assistance to ensure that the project
implements as planned; and (c) it will carry out central program activities that may affect more
than one project.
Composition Existing staff in Dikmenum, augmented as necessary by project-
financed consultants will be at the core of the management team.
Tasks, Roles and Responsibilities. The Director of Dikmenum will head the Central
Program Coordination Unit and coordinate the overall program of expanding junior secondary
education. An important function of the Central Program Coordination Unit will be to monitor
implementation achievements in the different provinces through progress reports and
monitoring indicators and to keep the Govemment and the Bank informed in this respect.
Dikmenum's role as the national level program coordination unit will be distinctly
different from the roles of the provincial (Kanwil) offices. As a program coordinator, its role
will be that of oversight and facilitation -- not implementation. Dikmenum will therefore: (a)
ensure that provincial implementation focuses on achieving their agreed project objectives as
measured through the agreed project-specific monitoring indicators; (b) assist the provinces in
establishing appropriate annual work plans and monitor that these are carried out in accordance
with agreed priorities, criteria and procedures; (c) help ensure timely preparation of regional
project budgets (DIP); (d) assist in establishing appropriate management information systems
(both financial and substantive) for use at the Kanwil level as well as at the National level; (e)
disseminate infornation to the provinces in order to highlight promising regional approaches in
the design of Annual Work Plans and Budgets; and (f) identify or provide technical assistance
to the provinces to ensure compliance with agreed project-specific criteria, rules and
regulations.

Annex 8: Page 4 of 6                          91
Linkages to the Provincial Teams. Dikmenum will also help design and implement
national and provincial capacity building plans as the project unfolds. This will include
assisting the provinces establish appropriate project information systems and procedures on the
principle that relevant information from one system will feed into the next system, and that
there will be meaningful back and forth flow of information across administrative levels.
Supervisory Functions. Dikmenum will have a procurement supervisory function to
help ensure that the project is implemented in accordance with the stipulations of the Loan
Agreement and the Bank's Procurement Guidelines. The CPCU will appoint procurement
specialists to provide training and technical assistance to PPIUs in the preparation of
procurement documents and to review documents from the provinces to ensure that they are in
line with project procurement guidelines.
Terms of Reference
Provincial Project Implementation Unit
Objectives. In order to get the project effectively implemented, the projects will be
executed through existing line authorities at the Kanwil level. There will be no expansion of
staff positions: existing positions will be used, and/or converted temporarily for project use as
and when necessary. Similarly, there will be no structural changes in the administrative
system.
Team, Organization and Responsibilities. Project-specific task forces, commensurate
with the focus and specificity of each of the provincial projects will be established as
necessary. Each of these task forces will be composed of existing staff at the Kanwil-level, and
-- where appropriate -- at the Kandep level. These task forces will grow and contract as
necessary during project implementation.
KaKanwil in each Province will be responsible for project implementation. To lend a
focal point to the proposed implementation arrangements, a Provincial Project Implementation
Unit (PPIU) under the overall leadership of KaKanwil, will be established in each Province.
This PPIU, managed by the head of the secondary education group (Kabid-Dikmenum) or a
Dikmenum staff member working under his/her supervision, will form the core of the proposed
implementation arrangement. The manager of the PPIU will be responsible for the day-to-day
management of the project and will report to KaKanwil on all matters pertaining to the project.
He/she will also keep the project authorities in Jakarta (Dikmenum) informed about progress,
including any events that threatened progress, such as the lack of human or financial resources
to resolve emerging problems. If necessary, one full-time monitoring officer and one or two
support staff will assist the manager of the PPIU in keeping track of implementation progress.
The PPIU will be assisted by a technical resource group in each province for the
duration of the project. The duties of this group, including that of consultants, will be to assist
in technical analysis of sector issues, prepare annual plans and detailed project designs,
troubleshoot on an ad-hoc basis, and conduct staff training/guidance. The technical resource
group will have access to other staff resources (including those from IKIPs/Universities) with
specialized project-related skills.

INDONESIA
Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project
PROJECT MONITORING AND EVALUATION
Introduction
1.    This Annex describes how project progress will be monitored and impact will be
evaluated. Reporting on monitoring and evaluation results will be done within the context of
Bank progress reporting. Project progress reporting will be on a semi-annual cycle. The
half-yearly progress report will cover the project status (physical progress. financial and
performance status) and will include status of the perforimanice indicators. The program
report will be prepared in advance of the half-yearly Batik supervisioni missions. Details of
progress reporting are described here.
Project Monitoring Indicators
2.     Project status will be monitored through performiance and output indicators (see
Annex 13). Project progress and performance will be monitored on the basis of several types
of indicators:
(a)    Progress Monitoring Agaitist the Implementation Plan
Annex 12 (Implementation Plan) gives hinformationi on the milestones, dates
and tasks to be carried out during the implementation process. At each
supervision mission, these will be reviewed, their status assessed, and the
Plan updated to reflect progress. The updated version of the lmplementation
Plan would indicate actual versus planned  ilmplementation and slhow
adjustments made accordingly.
(b)    Ouantitative Indicators
In each progress report and at each supervision, the indicators as listed in
Annex 10 will be reviewed. The actual against planned data will be filled out
by the PlUs, and will be discussed during supervision.
(c)    Qualitative Indicators
At each supervision mission, an evaluation will be undertaken by the
supervision team of progress regarding qualitative indicators. The PlUs will
mention, in the progress report, the status of these indicators, which will then
form the basis for the supervision discussion. For each indicators, specific
evidence of progress should be provided.



Annex 9: Page 2 of 2                     95
Format of Progress Report
3.    The half-yearly progress report should be organized in the following way and should
include the following sections:
(a)    A narrative section describing progress of each of the components: Issues
that have arisen, problems, delays, suggested changes, decisions made in the
last year should be included for each relevant component.
(b)    An update of the indicators: Actual status against planned implementation,
quantitative and qualitative indicators.
(c)    A section listing the recommendations and agreements made during the last
Bank supervision mission and actions or present position.
(d)    A list of all contract activities expected during the next year and half-year.
(e)    A set of tables covering the following:
* Expenditures to date (SAR plan and current status) shown by category of
expense and by component and status of civil works (number of
contracts, location, type of school covered and cost for each contract);
* Training activities (SAR plan and current status): number and type of
training courses, number of participants, duration of each course, cost of
each course;
*  Status of Technical Assistance contracts (foreign and local): number of
person/months planned and currently used, costs of contracts and status
of disbursement.
(f)    A report from the auditing consultants (whenever available).
Additional Reports and Reviews
4.    A few special reviews will also be carried out during project implementation, to
further support monitoring of progress. These reviews will be carried out by independent
external consultants and will be managed by the Project Coordination Unit in Dikmenum.
(a)    Survey, in preparation for mid-term, to evaluated progress in improving
management skills and processes in Kanwils and Kandeps;
(b)    Annually, a review of expenditures of government funds by private schools;
(c)    Annually, monitoring of the effectiveness of the scholarship fund selection
and distribution; and
(d)    At mid-term, a review of the effectiveness of the contract teachers program.
Supervision Schedule
5.    Supervision missions will be held twice a year, during the project implementation
period. A planned mission schedule is listed in Annex 11. The mid-term review mission will
consist of a more thorough review; this mission will review overall progress since the
beginning of the implementation period, and will evaluate the need for adjustments or
restructuring of the project for the remainder of the project period.
